; The new Mayor is sure to have a warm
time over the distribution of the spoils of
victory. There are forty applicants for
every, place at his disposal. He is bound
to make thirty-nine political enemies
every time that he signs a commission.
He has been considering the . advisability
of appointing a full set of Commissioners
for every board and giving the appointees
a chance. to go into court and make legal
battle for possession of the goods. On re
flection he decided to test his power to
displace . existing Commissioners by ap
pointing a brand-new Board of Education.
It was reasoned that Mayor Phelan was
particularly unfortunate in his selection
of Educational Commissioners and that
the public would regard . nearly any
change in the board as a change for the
better. It was considered further that the
members of the board by their own con
duct in office had Intensified the public
displeasure which was provoked when
Mayor Phelan commissioned them to look
after common school affairs. The Mayor
elect is sure that the public would com
mend him for making a clean sweep of
this board, hence it would not be surpris
ing If the test of bis power should be
made In this direction.

**^ The Incoming Mayor had made hl|" cal
culations to step in oh Monday, January
6,. at high,, noon. It may please him to
signify ' noon 'of Wednesday, January S,
as an acceptable time. If Mr. Schmitz
were a Democrat of the Jacksonian school
he would , rejoice at the opportunity of
taking office on January 8, as that day is
the anniversary "of General Jackson'3
great victory over the British at New
Orleans. The old Democratic campaign
refrain was:
"Where was Andrew Jackson then?
He was up to his neck in mud and blood.
Fighting the battles of his countrymen.
Notwithstanding the opinion of the City
and County Attorney, the offlcers^elect of
the Union Labor party, headed by Mayor
Schmitz, will demand their offices on
Monday. Under decisions of the Supreme
Court this seems to be the only safe plan
for them to pursue, as that court has hehl
in a number of cases that where an offi
cial falls to demand his office at the time
at which he rightfully believes it to be
due, then he forfeits his claim to it. It
is â¢ not the desire for two extra days of
office that is actuating them In this move-
â¢ The City Attorney decides that- the offi
cers chosen at the last municipal elec
tion will not go into office until the be
ginning' of January 8. In other words, the
present officers who were inducted into
office January 8, 1900, were elected to
serve two years and that their term of
service will not expire until the last hour
of January 7, 1902. Coming down to a
fine point, Mayor-elect Eugene E. Schmitz
is not entitled to succeed Mayor Phelaa
until 12:01 a. m. .Wednesday, January S.
It is presumed that the Incoming Mayor
will cheerfully await the dawn before dis
turbing Mayor Phelan, but the latter
nay find it more convenient to surrender
the works shortly after midnight than to
rise early after dawn.

Sad Accident Occurs in Yuba. County
Resulting in Wheatland Man's
-Beatt. 3-f-i'ls?
MARYSVILLE, Jan. 2!â Samuel Enochs,
who was accidentally shot, while hunting
deer in the foothills nine miles east of
Wheatland, in Yuba County, on. New
Year's day, died at his. home at that
place this morning. Details of the acci
dent show it to have been another case of
a hunter mistaking a friend for the
hunted - animal.
Enochs was one -of a party that went
deer hunting Wednesday morning. Jeff
Thomas started up a deer and was fol
lowing it He saw. something moving in
the brush in front of him, and, thinking
it was the deer, raised his rifle and flred.
The ball struck Enochs In the left thigh.
An operation was performed as soon as
possible, but the wounded man was too
weak from loss of blood jto survive the
shock. He leaves a wife and two grown
daughters. On New Year's day, Just a
year ago, Enoch's brother was killed at
Tulare. ;

These competitive examinations are to be
conducted by a paid examination board of
three or five competent persons not Identified
Â¦with the school department and never serving
In succession; selected, two by the Superin
tendent, two by the Board of Education, and
these to select a fifth; applicants to be known
to the examiner by number, and all papers
written by applicants to become public docu
ments and subject to review for at least three
months. Lists of all applicants arranged - in
the order of their rank, who attained the re
quired minimum, are to be furnished the
Board of Education, from which teachers may
be selected in the order of their , standin-
These examinations should be held at lealt
annually and new lists thus prepared. â¢"""â¢
The foregoing plan I submit excludes con
eideration of any financial, political, social or
friendly influence that an applicant may pos
sess. It will bar politics from the operation
of our schools in their educational functions
end thus remove an objectionable influence
from teachers, pupils and educational adminis
tration. Â¦
It will relieve school officials of the Im
portunities of applicants and their friends and
<-nablÂ« them to devote much -more time to their
duties; for it is safe to assert that nearly one
third of the time of a school official who haj<
patronage to distribute is consumed i n en'er
tainlng requests for place. Not only will this
time be 6Â»ved to the school official " but his
mind, undisturbed by consideration of appii
cations for place, can be devoted with its un
impaired strength to the task of studying
school conditions and problems. ' -
We rourt Â«Jo one thins or the other in regard
to the teaching service of the public schools
We may let politicians of high and low decree'
neighbors and friends, push people, good bad
or Indifferent, Into - teachers' positions â¢'â¢ and
Tilth this result; that there- will be no progress
In scholarship or general culture. Â¦ nor anv
growth In character, disciplinary power or
teaching ability. . Or we may guard admissions
making the standard high and eliminate any
considerations of "Influence"; train beginners
promote on the baEis of merit; dismiss those
who fail in the test for capacity; destroy
sycophancy: encourage Independent - manhood
Â»nd : womanhood : Instill professional pride: ln
epire progress whose benign . influence will be
felt In the home; give enduring support to good
citizenship â better life to the commonwealth. . Â¦
Appointment of Judges.
I submit, however, that the original selec
tion should be left to the judgment of no single
board or school official. . The responsibility is
too great. In a town or large city a Super
intendent's time would be completely consumed
by the reception of aprllcants and their friends,
the consideration of their credentials and by
the investigation of their -qualifications. The
Superintendent or school official does not live
who, unbiased by considerations of political,
social or other influences, can select the best
qualified persons from all those who apply.. To
declare that from all who make application a
board can select a limited number, and those
the best, is to arrogate and to assume om
niscience, an attribute which is ascribed to
the Deity alone. Again to declare that one
is not and will not be influenced by considera
tions of friendship in the selection of teachers
from those who possess a parity of qualification
is to declare oneself an ingrate.
Therefore I repeat that I would delegate the
Â£rave responsibility of originally selecting
teachers to the Judgment of no one school
official or to a Board of Education. The Gov
ernment of our republic inaugurated civil ser
vice reform In the selection of Its subordinate
employes. The system is being extended into
the government of our great municipalities.
Why not adopt it in the great business of se
lecting teachers In our cities and counties? I
FUggest this as a plan:
For the purpose of their employment let the
teacher* of a School Department of any city
or county be divided into three . sections.
First, the secondary or high school section:
second, the elementary or grammar and pri
mary section; third, the section of special
teachers. Ail applicants in any section must
be possessed of valid certificates entitling them
to teach in that section (it is here assumed
that the possession of a teacher's certificate
1* a gnarantee, under the law, of good char
acter), be subjected to competitive examina
tions whose scope shall be broad, embracing
the history, science and art of education and
subjects required to be taught in that section
of school to which they apply for admission
All candidates who fail to reach a minimum
percentage in any one subject or minimum
average in all are to be rejected. This will
insure good scholarship, the prime essential of
a teacher; and the epectacle of one unable to
eolve- mathematical problems, answer gram
matical, historical, geographical or scientific
ttuestions within the range of their teaching
work will be absent." â¢
Difficulty of Selection.
Notwithstanding the- fact that it centralizes
power, experience has resulted In making the
third method â election by the board of edu
cation on recommendation by the superintend
ent â a rule of conduct in many cities of the
United States, and it can be stated that this
method is steadily gaining favor. The main
reason assigned is that the average member of
a board of education has neither time, oppor
tunity nor ability to make such Investigations
or apply such tests as are necessary to de
termine the fitness of teachers. The superin
tendent on the other hand is, by the nature of
his office, 'and generally by his training, bet
ter qualified for this function. Again it fixes
responsibility. If the schools are not pro
gressive and thorough the blame can be imme
diately charged to a superintendent, providing
he be vested with power and unhampered in
1U exercise.
Schools erist not to please contractors and
place-hunters, but to supply instruction; to
brine into activity and to' direct in the proper
mental, moral and physical avenues the dor
mant and developing" powers or the child. The
readiness to accept almost anything that passes
under the name of instruction has done much
to retard the development of schools. Yet It
IB gratifying to note the closer discrimination
with which people are observing what is done.
\Vhen they consider the diCering values of in
struction, they come to the all-important prob
lem of the organization and supervision of the
teaching- force. Fine buildings, complete text
books and . perfectly equipped laboratories are
great aids in education, but without good
teachers they yield unsatisfactory results. Tht:
teacher is the principal factor in a successful
school. . . .
Kow should the teacher be selected and ap
pointed? Three methods are ordinarily em
ployed. First, orgins.1 selection .by a board
of education; second, election by a board, .of
education on recommended selections by its
committee appointed for that purpose; third,
election by a board of education on recommend
ed selections made by ; the superintendent of
schools. Much can be said in favor of ,aud
against each system, for each possesses its ad
vantages and disadvantages.'

To-day was certainly the busiest as well
As. .the most important of the # convention.
All the big guns and many of the lesser
ones as well were heard during the day,
the subjects throughout being highly
technical. As a result brains to-night are
near to bursting with tne amount of in
formation they- have been called upon to
absorb since 9 o'clock this morning.
. After .the addresses by President An
drews of Nebraska University and Presi
dent Jordan of Stanford probably the
most important and certainly the most in
teresting to Calitornians was the discus
sion of the two topicsâ "The Selection and
Appointment of Teachers" and "The Pres
ent Duty of the State Touching the
Training of Its Kiementary and Secon
dary Teachers."
The first of these was opened by Dr.
Andrews, who spoke in general terms, ad
vocating the making ot a list of eligible
teachers by a system of competitive ex
aminations that should be absolutely fair
and honest, and after that a careful se
lection from this list of the best person
for the particular place. He also spoke
strongly in favor of placing such appoint
ive power in the hands ot one person of
undoubted qualifications, rather than in
those of a committee or" board of'educa
tion, in order to definitely fix the respon
sibility.
Benjamin Ide Wheeler, who closed the
argument, -expressed himself emphatically
in favor of this method. Two well-known
San Franciscans spoke upon this subject â
City Superintendent R. H. Webster and
C. W. Mark of the San Francisco Board

SAN JOSE, Jan. 2.â A ghastly discovery
was made by section men on the "harrow
gauge, railroad between this city and
Campbell shortly after 7 o'clock this
morning. In a smoldering pile of ashes
was found the charred remains of a hu
man body burned beyond all possible
recognition. Surrounding 1 the corpse were
the ashes of a small pile of railroad ties,
which had been left along the track. " The
fire had nearly burned Itself out when di3-s
covered and nothing but the blackened
trunk and charred head of the man re
main to tell the story.
The Coroner and Sheriff were notified
and an investigation seemed to show that
some unknown man, perhaps a tramp, had
taken this method of transit to the be
yond. A revolver, found near by, had one
cartridge indented. Frank Mendoza heard
a shot flred about 1 o'clock and earlier
saw a tramp sleeping at the spot- The fire
and gun were .doubtless the double means
for suicide.
Upon returning to their rooms Luck"
hardt found that the documents of -their
estate and other letters and papers were
gone and he redoubled the search, calling

ance. While these papers are not nego
tiable, their loss may involve title to the

"I am convinced that she was induced

."I have , telegraphed to my sisters in

she had but 70 cents in money."
Luckhardt began an immediate search
for his sister. He traced her to a dress
making establishment, where she re
mained from 11 until 12:30 o'clock, when
she went out, telling the dressmaker that
there was an important engagement she
must keep at 1:30 o'clock. There all trace
of the girl is lost.
Last Tuesday morning Miss Luckhardt
left home, an expressman coming for her
trunk. When Luckhardt returned Tues
day evening *he found a short note ad
dressed to him, in which his sister stated
that she would no longer be a burden on
him and as she was convinced that he did
not like her she would go away and try
to find employment and make her -own
living. She told- her landlady that she
was going for a visit, of two or three
weeks with friends in this city and from
there would probably go : to San Fran
cisco, where she has a sister. Another
sister lives in Alameda: :.-.;

Juana, in Lower California. The Luck
hardts are descendants of one of Califor
nia's oldest families, -their maternal -vuncle

utor of the joint estate of brother and

he could place her in school or could find
a home that seemed suitable for his sister

week. ] representing the University of Cali
fornia, at the meetings of the American

Scarcity of testimony from passengers

passengers had not been subpenaed bv
his deputies. His sharp Injunction to
Deputy McCormick to "dig up" some of

there had been a general slaughtering of

were already beneath the sod. Â¦
Â¦ Mayor-elect Schmitz was a witness. He
told of s^ing the cars crash together: of
telephoning from his residence on Fill
more street to the Market Street Railway
offices, telling them of the accident and
asking them to send physicians, and of his
jroing to the asslstanceof the Injured.
Concerning the mechanical appliances the
Mayor-elect said he had paid particular
attention to them and deemed them ab
solutely safe. He said that if, the pre
scribed precautions were observed by. em
ployes accident would be impossible. He
had not known of any previous mishap on
the gravity line.
Motorman Deutschmann furnished .the
one dramatic Incident of/ the Inquest. He
was telllncr on the stand that he heard the
"ko ahead" bells, started on the "first
notch." heard the conductor shout "stop,"
and did stop the car. He said he looked
about and saw the conductor in the rear
of the enclosed portion of the car and
heard him explain to the passengers that
they would be transferred at Vallejo
street. As Deutschmann told - this ' he
arose to show the conductor's attitude
and, overcome with the recollection of
what followedâ the wild. ride down the hill,
the collision and the screams of- the
women and cries and ' groans of 'Â¦- the ' in
jured â the motorman â¢ sank back Â¦ into - his
seat and covered his face with his hands,
sobbing, his body trembling with emotion.

juries in the Fillmore-street rail
way accident on the evening of Decem
ber 23, returned a verdict that the wreck
was due to the carelessness of Timer
Alexander Cameron and Motorman A. T.
Deutschmann. The conductor, M. P.
Phelps, who failed to couple the car to
the grip on, the cable, escaped censure
because the jury believed that the testi
mony, though conflicting, snowed that
the timer rang the signal bells, indicating
that the car was attached to me cable
and ready to start, a duty tnat oeiongea
to the conauctor alone ana snould not
have been assumed by the tinier.
The testimwny demonstrated clearly
that the street car company's failure to
enrorce strictly ivs regulations as to the
duties ot aurerent employes at tne point
of danger maae the accident possible.
Mechanical appliances that Insure abso
lute safety on the gravity section oÂ£ tne
electric road were rendered inoperative
by a careless confusion of duties on the
part of two or three men.
The testimony was" not complete, as but
few passengers were placed on the stand,
and many direct contradictions left mucn
doubt as to the occurrences Immediately
prior to the wild ride down the hill. The
timer, the motorman and the signalman
an testified positively tnat they heard the
conductor's "go ahead' 1 bells. If they rang
the motorman had no duty but to obey
and switch on the current that would
send the car over the brow of the hfll.
But the conductor denied that he gave
the "go ahead" signal, and two passen
gers, who claimed to have been watching
him, say that he did not. It was three
against three, and the jury apparently
accepted the testimony of the conductor
and passengers, for it censured the ,mo
torman and had no word of condemna
tion for the conductor, who, after receiv
ing his orders from the timer, turned the
trolley and mounted the car again, leav
ing it uncoupled from 'v the cable, while he
explained to . the passengers that they
were to stay aboard, be taken back to
Vallejo street and transferred there to a
car that would take them on southward,
while his car would return, to the bay.
He alone of the three had knowledge that
the car was not attached to the cable
and that he would have to dismount
again and make the coupling before the
car could safely start down the steep in
cline.

REDDING, Jan. 2.â Joseph Kelly, one of
the early miners of this county, gave his
gold claim and his cabin to a wood
hauler named Thompson, who this morn
ing saved his life. For years Kelly, who
is 73 years of age, has mined a profitable
claim in Oregon Gulch, six miles from
Redding, where he lived alone. Yesterday
he set ourto prospect In the vicinity. As
night came on the old man lost his way
A steady rain began and he was lost in
the woods. He walked blindly until all
but exhausted, fearing to lie down lest
be ehould sleep and never awaken.
Early this morning, when he had lost
hope. Thompson, the wood-hauler, found
him. ajid tooK him ip, revived him and se
cured medical attention. The grateful old
miner says he is done with mining and re
warded his preserver with the gift of his
mine and cabin.

ah officer of the State Department whose
attention was called to the dispatch stat
ing that the Ban Righ had been armed
and equipped as a war vessel and is now
on her way to Venezuela- The Ban Righ
cannot be classed as a pirate. Secretary

States could not regard as piratical ves
sels manned by parties in arms against
the Government has been accepted by all
maritime nations. The Ban High will
therefore, not be recognized as a pirate

be Eeized, then President Castro will
probably be aJlowed to Â¦wreak his will
upon the Venezuelans on board, though
the British Government will undoubtedly
be able to save any of Its subjects who
may be captured.

As a result of the financial embarrass
ment of the Everett-Moore syndicate a
run was started on the Dime Savings and
Banking Company, of which Everett and
Moore are directors, late to-day. All de
mands were promptly met, and President
Watterson said the bank had ample funds
on hand to pay all depositors who desired
their money. The institution is one of
the city depositories.. "When its last re
port was issued in October the bank's to
tal resources were placed at $5,762,007, in
cluding a surplus fund of $335,0ju.
Personally I have had no connection with
and no knowledge cf the operations of the
Kverett-Moore syndicate. Although I am
named as one of the members of the commit
tee for the reorganization of their affairs, I
have now no actual knowledge of their con
dition except as informed by another - com
mittee of Cleveland bankers, which has spent
more than a week in making an . investiga
tion. While 1 have taken no part in this
investigation, the bankers who conducted It
are among our safe > and conservative men and
their report should inspire' confldence. The
entire affairs of the syndicate are now under
going a thorough investigation i with - the as-'
sistance of chartered accountants, and all per
sons concerned Ehould, in my Judgment, feel
assured that the interests of all classes of
creditors will be conserved bv competent bust
ness management at the hands of the commit
tee chosen. â¢â¢. . . â¢ . * Â¦

TACOMA, Jan. 2.â Local business in
every line Is being augmented by the sud
den paying out this week, commencing to
day, of $500,000 in gold coin to the de
positors of the Metropolitan Bank. The
money has been advanced by the Vander
bilt family of New York to save the fam
ily name and prevent poor people from
losing their eavings through the manage
ment of the bank by two Vanderbilt
nephews.
Payment was begun to-day by Charles
McNamee, business manager for George
Vanderbilt. The depositors exceed 3000.
of whom about 800 are school children.
Those having certificates of deposit are
being paid off at the London and San
Francisco Bank and book accounts at the
National Bank of Commerce.

So interested are the Chinese in the
matter that they purpose erecting a Joss
house in honor of Dr. Wong Woo. They
consider him a second Aesculapius, ' and
have been showering honors upon him for
some time. Lew Fook. according to re
ports, can go back to his business without
further fear of molestation. Dr. Wong
Woo has apparently solved one of the
problems of the highbinder war.
Dr. Wong T/oo triumphantly exhibits
the poultice-extracted bullet as a proof of
his skill. Foo Que Lee and Jo Due, who
were shot at the same time, also came
under the doctor's ministrations and were
cured by the same methods.
He came to me with four .wounds in hia
body. One of them was very dangerous. It
was in the same spot where McKinley was
shot. The doctors out at the City Hall could
not find the bullet. I put a plaster on it, and
two days after the bullet came out. Now he
is entirely well. Once before, in Sebastopol.
I had a similar case. Jew Wan How was shot
in the back and his case was given up by the
local physicians. . I put on my plaster and
pulled him through. At the same time How
Lue was shot in the ankle, and the bone was
shattered; now ha walks as well as ever he did.
My plaster found the bullet, Lee Fook,,. Jew
Wall How and How Lue are well men.
cures him of his injuries is considered far
above the ordinary. Dr. Wong Woo tells
a peculiar story, original in its Oriental
flavorings, as to the way he cured Lew
Fook of his wounds. He said:

ing night. The other is Dr.
Wong Woo., 764 Clay street, who, after a
futile operation had been performed at
the Central Emergency Hospital, located
and extracted the bullet that perforated
Lew Fook's stomach. Dr. Wong Woo
brought him back to health and strength
and is dividing the honors with the in
jured man.
Lew Fook's condition was considered bo
serious that his dying statement was
taken by the police. He was for a long
time considered one of the most desirable
marks for highbinder bullets in China
town, as he was the proprietor of one of
the most prosperous gambling games in
that quarter. He was attacked that night
in 4he club at 104 Waverly place and shot
four times. Some of his wounds were
superficial, hut the most dangerous
wound was In the stomach.
This wound is what gives Lew Fook
his prominence. When a Chinese high
binder is shot at once, though his wounds
may be many, his life is sacred there
after. If he lives through the ordeal, none
there is who may do him harm.

BOSTON, Jan. 2.-In the six-day bicycle
race seme of the best racing of the week
was witnessed to-night. Each of the six
leading teams contributed to the excite
ment. The pace was fierce, but not one
of the leaders was able to score on any
other than the tail-enders.

The Board of Fire Commissioners, act
ing on the recommendation of Chief Sul
livan, decided at yesterday's meeting to
sbolish the whistles which have hitherto
announced the breaking out of-fires. The
jiew order goes Into effect on Monday.
The pioneer bell, which for so many
years pealed forth its alarm from the old
<;ity Hall and later from Brenham place,
and the bell at Sixteenth and FoLsora
streets are not to be silenced.

Rabbi Meyers Lectures.
Yesterday afternoon before the Council
of Jewish Women in the vestry room of
Temple EimrJiu-El a lecture on "The
Jewish Woman" was delivered by Rabbi
Isidore Myers. In the course of the lec
ture he contrasted the degraded condi
tion of women arming other races of an
tiquity and the exalted position that
Â¦women occupied in the Jewish race both
in biblical times and more especially
during the several centuries of rabbinical
development. He said that the Jewish
v.oman was invested by the rabbis with
the holiest of missionsâ the mission of
love. He pointed out her rights and
duties as a wife, as a. mother, as the
angel of charity and as the' priestess of
her home. Woman was spoken of by the
rabbis in terms of the greatest tender
ness, gracefulness and purity- AVomen
like angels were made the messengers of
peace and love, of the subllmest thought
mid the noblest sentiments. Every age,
according to the rabbis, can only be re
generated through tbe influence of its
noble women. The founder of the Greek
race was said to have been saved by a
Koat. The founder of the Roman nation
was saved by a she wolf. Moses, the
founder of the Jewish nation, was saved
hy his mother. The preservation of the
Jewish race during many centuries of
persecution is chiefly due to the devotion
of the Jewish woman to her God, to her
husband, to her children and to her home,
which she kept pure and godly and con
verted Into a sanctuary.

PASADENA, Jan. 2.â Left Guard "Ted
dy" Roosevelt of the Stafford football
team and Left Tackle William Traeger
are confined to their beds at the Hotel
Green to-night. Roosevelt was found to
have sustained a fracture of his left leg
and had two ribs broken in the game of
yesterday. He played on. without saying
anything about it.
To-night he has a. high fever, the result
of attending the Glee Club concert on
crutches when he saould have been in bed.
Traeger, with several ligaments in his
shoulder broken, did the same. He is not
expected to be out for five weeks. Roose
velt will be confined to his room for two
months.

The Commissioners also Â¦ ordered | the
construction, of ' a footpath on the west
side of the great highway leading -to' the
cliff and beach. .Hitherto in entering the
park from the ocean side the pedestrians
have been in doubt as : to where to walk.
The only road, for a considerable distance
into tha park has had no footpath and
the pedestrians were kept continually
dodging horses, automobiles and bicycles.
The. board has found that the water. in
the valley from which the. park supply
has- been pumped is in danger, of being
exhausted. To augment the supply, the
Commissioners decided to erect "a wind-,
mill directly back of the .Government life
saving station on the beach, where it has
been found that a flow of 20,000 gallons of
fresh water an hour can.be obtained. ;
For the convenience of the tennis play
ers the board ordered four shower baths
to be built at the tennis courts.
The board decided to have a tunnel dug
on the north' side of the park similar to
the one leading from the terminus of the
Haight-street car line." This tunnel, will
enable the public to enter the grand
court without passing over the bridge,
which Is' usually crowded with vehicles. Â¦..

the season will be a colonial ball given by
the local members of the George Wash
ington Memorial Association. At a meet
ing -of the State association in the Cen
tury Club building yesterday morning it
was decided to hold this ball some time In
February, the proceeds of which will go
toward" California's quota of $10,000.
. It is the intention to erect a memorial
building in .the new- national university
which will cost half a million dollars:
Sarah â¢Â£>.. Hamlin, the State chair
man, presided over . the meeting and
named committees to complete arrange
ments, ' fix the date for the ball and .- ap
point patronesses.

Belmont the Regular Nominee.
NEW YORK. Jan. 2.â Justice Scott of
the Supreme Court , to-day handed down
a decision declaring Perry Belmont to be
the regular Democratic nominee for Con
gress in the Seventh New York District.
Colonel Asa Bird Gardiner called on Can
didate Belmont this evening and assured
him of the support of Tammany Hall,
some of whose leaders had opposed him.
The regular committee of the First As
sembly District has decided to support
him and so have delegates from the regu
lar organization in the Second and Third
districts.

LEAVENWORTH, Kans.. Jan. 2.â The
most novel court which ever sat in Kan
sas convened in the library of the United
States penitentiary this afternoon for the
arraignment of the ringleaders of the mu
tiny of November 7. who are implicated
In the killing of Guard. J. B. Waldrupe.
Every person in the court, with but one
exception, was either a United States offi
cial or a prisoner wearing the stripes de
noting desperate characters.
The court convened at 3 p. m.,'with
United States Commissioner Bond on the
bench, Ed McKeever, assistant United
States Attorney, representing the Gov
ernment, with E. A. Prescott, deputy
marshal.
The mutineers, seventeen in number,
were brought in, each wearing the ball
and chain, and after a consultation of the
officers the following men were arraigned
on the charge of murder:"
Turner Barnes, Bob Clark, Frank
Thompson, Gilbert Mullin and Fred Rob
inson. Four others, who have not yet
been captured, are included in the
charges. â¢ S
The warrants were served by Deputy
Marshal Prescott, and upon being ar
raigned each of the prisoners entered a
plea of not guilty. The trial. will be held
in the prison library at 3 o'clock to-mor
row afternoon. . / . -
Warden McClaughrey denies the re
ports sent out that guards are resigning
for fear of their lives.
Canal Prospects Brighten.
MANAGUA, Jan. 2.â The Congress of
Nicaragua assembled last night and Pres
ident Zelaya read his message, inform
ing the House of the signing of the pro
tocol regarding- leasing the proposed canal
route to the United States. The President
declared that the prospects of building
the canal were becoming brighter and
that the United States formally guaran
teed the sovereignty of Nicaragua.

WASHINGTON, Jan. 2.â The dinner to
the Cabinet, the first of the large formal
functions of the year at the White House,
was given by the . President and Mrs.
Roosevelt to-night. A large number, of
persons were invited to meet the mem
bers of the Cabinet, the guests including
Senators and Representatives in Con
gress and some friends of tne President.
The Marine Band furnished the music.
Those present, in addition to the President
and Mrs. Roosevelt, were: .
. Drouet wrung success out ; of the un-,
promising role of Armand. He gave a
rendering of the. part- conspicuous , for
cumulative power, passion, sympathy and
convincing quality. The part of Mons. l)u
valwas admirably sustained ,by, Carl
Ahrendt: H. S. Northrup was a slfflclenlly
vicious De Varville to receive the ac
knowledgement of hisses from the audi
ence: Charles Cherry was a charming Xel
low as Gaston; Mrs. E. A. Eberle gave a
good but rather comic valentiny of Mme.
Prudence;. Margaret- Moffatt as Nichctte
showed spirit and originality; Amy.Ric
ard did all that was necessary as Nanlne,
and Amy Rankin, a local light, was
Olympe. . - â¢
1 But her after success, as the repentant
Camille. more . particularly in the scene
with Armand's fatherâ as purely, artistic
and pathetic bit of ' acting as has been
seen for a long day : on the local stage -
was Instant and unquestioned. After the
gambling scene, where Robert Drouet, as
Armand. shared honors with the star, the
curtain was lifted. again, and again to the
insistent demand of the delighted audi
ence, who succeeded at last in getting the
actors before the curtain -after a. little
matter of ten or twelve recalls. ..
Her first scene in "Camille" promised a
flat failure. Arch," gay, innocently allur
ing, she might have been Â¦.: anything
rather than the creature she .was attempt
ing to depict. The canker in the charac
ter -was -not there,- the erotic- suggestion,
the subtle atmosphere of corruption that
Bernhardt suggested bo powerfully. It
is a constitutional lack. Miss Mannering
was not born that way. with that kind of
vision, that kind .of 'speech. It is out of
her. emotional gamut, . pitched out of her
compass, a foreign tongue to her. .
I A thing' or two does come our way now
and again, even if San j Francisco Is . at
the end of the , world to the . theatrical
manager. ;~ One came yesterday, in Mary
Mannering's first' adventure into the emo-,
tlonal ' domain of ."Camille." one of the
most Interesting 1 dramatic- experiments
that we have y^t had the, chance to see
tried here. The role, so far as was known
of . Miss â¢ Manneringr's work, ; was | entirely
foreign to all her previous efforts, the
character, the whole atmosphere of the
play essentially, different- from anything
with which she had before been concerned.
A role essentially subtle, complex,, de
manding large emotional power, the tragic
note, 'the erotic key, Â¦ was â¢ not' the sort of
part' that one found it easy to flt Mis*
Mannerlng into. Even among her faithr
fuleet followers-^and the r Columbia was
full of them yesterday â there were I those
who prophesied failure â¢ for, the young
.beauty who has taken the town by storm.
XnÂ© others, of course, were. ready; to bet
their. 1 best boots on her success In any
thing she attempted. ' :
It fell out, however; that both were right
â and Â¦ wrong. . As far as a -woman with an
atmosphere as pure as a new-blown -rose
could suggest the Parisian cocotte, skilled'
in all the vicious arts of .unlawful allure
ment; sad, with .the horrible cynicism
born* of her dreadful knowledge; glad,
with a gayety sadder than' tears; and
strong in the frightful philosophy- her life
had taught. Just so far was Mary Man
nering successful as Camille: That is, not
very far, on that side of the character.
The repentant Camille Is another story,
for here Miss Mannering achieved' an un
qualified success. To. say that the actress
displayed strong emotional power, an easy
perfection of technique, a delicate finish,
restraint; naturalness, classic grace and,
more than all, an Irresistible pathos that
set men sniffing and women to sobbing
outright, this would be to mention only
the beginning- -of her accomplishment.
There is everything here for a Juliet, an
Imogene, â¢ an. Ophelia, a Viola, and Miss
Mannerlng is wasting her sweetness' on
Janice 'Merediths and 'Camilles. True,
there is an infinite deal more of her ,in
"Camille" than in the other role, but
neither get the best that she has to give,
for it is infinitely too good. for either.

Contest for Leadership
of Ohio Legislature
Grows Tense.
Lines Are Now Drawn on
Old Question of Local
Option.
COLUMBUS, Ohio. Jan. 2.â The contest
between Republican factions over the or
ganization of the Legislature is in doubt
so far as the House is concerned. Th3
claims of either faction would indicate
that one element is confident of Price
becoming Speaker and the other of Mc-
Kennon, but the efforts of both show that
there are yet "votes to get" by whomso
ever becomes Speaker. The list of un
pledged members haSj however, been re
duced to-day to a half dozen. These six
are said to have made no pledge* to either
fide, and it Is conceded that they hold the
balance of power.
There was much work done to-day from
the homes of Senator Hanna in Wash
ington. Senator Foraker in Cincinnati.
Congressman Dick in Akron. Congress
man Taylor in Columbiana and others,
especially at Toledo, where Representa
tive Denman is st'll considered by some
ts doubtful.
There was much communication with
all the counties In which doubtful mem
bers reside.
The most prominent arrival was that of
George B. Cox, the Cincinnati leader, who
Insists that he Is as "good a friend of
Senator Hanna as ever," but that he was
pledged to Price for Speaker before the
Junior Ohio Senator broke with Price and
that he is here now to do all he can for
Price.
The leadership of the antl-Hanna side
was conceded to Cox on his arrival.
It is claimed that the developments to
day indicate that Congressman Taylor of
the old McKinlev district will likely be
Â«m opponent of Congressman Dick for the
next Republican gubernatorial nomination
and that Taylor from his home Is work-
Ing as hard for Price as Dick is working
for McKir.r.cn.
The lines have been distinctly drawn
on the old question of local option. The
liQUor associations have representatives
here for Price and the Ohio Anti-Slater
League for McKlnnon. In the last Leg
islature Price opposed the Clark local op
tion bill and McKinnon supported It.
The Senate standing committees are se
lected by the Senators themselves and
the House committees are all named uy
the Sj>eakei\ Â¦

PERSONAL MENTION.
The Philippine tariff bill went through the House
after a few hours' debate, but it is said the Senators
are going, to use it to exercise their oratory on, and
the chances are they will keep it. on hand nearly all
winter.
The commercial editor- of a Boston paper in not
ing the trade conditions of that city says: "The in
nate resiliency of the market will soon develop into
kinetic buoyancy." It is just the same here, though
of course we would uot.^ay it that way .
ThÂ« promptness with which the Panama Canal
Company reduced the price of their canal from
$109,000,000 to $40,000,000 ib a proof that they are
the kind of men who will not let a few dollars stand
in the way of a trade, and it is evident we have not
yet heard the last of them.
It is said Chauncey Depew went to France to get
married without taking with him .all the evidence
concerning his birth and a lot of other things which
French law requires, and at the last minute he found
his wedding blocked and had to apply, to the United
States Government to help him out.

A report from New York states that even at this
early date all staterooms, cabins and' other choice
places in the steamers between that city and Europe
are engaged for the early summer by persons eager
to get over in time for the corcfriation, so it will be
seen America will be well represented at the show.
At the present time Democracy has three military
men suggested as candidates for- the Presidency,
Dewey, Schley and Miles; but it is to be noted that
Mr. Bryan has resumed his activity and will deliver
speeches in various parts of the country during the
coming winter, so perhaps the colonel will save-his
party from the necessity of choosing between the
general and the admirals. - *
Judging by , his past record there -is every", reason
for expecting Palma to- lay the. foundations of Cuban
government firmly and justly.', 'He was. prominent in
Under any circumstances it is to be regretted that
the first contest in the island should have resulted in i
the withdrawal of one party before the election took
place. There is in it' an implied menace to appeal
from the ballot to the ' sword. V. So long as the United.
States troops remain in the island there is' no dan
ger: of such ah appeal; but t^ey .must be withdrawn
sooner or â¢ later, and - it will; t^en behoove -the newly
elected President to see to jt that order is maintained
and any effort at civil, disturbance promptly sup
pressed. Palma enters, therefore, upon a difficult']
task, and merits American , sympathy in every- well
directed effort to accomplish Â¦â¢ ii with success.
] One of the curious features of the situation is that
the successful candidate has been living, in the
United States while the campaign was at its hottest
A New York dispatch announces that Palma was
seen on the night after the election "in his home in
Central Valley, N. Y.," and announced that his aim
as President of Cuba would be' to .strengthen the
pleasant relations that now exist between Cuba and
the United States. .It is somewhat surprising that
the successful candidate should have remained in this
country instead of going to Cuba to lead his follow
ing, particularly, as the opposition to him was based
upon the charge that he is more subject to "American
than to" Cuban influences. It would seem that he had
no doubt about ..the iesult, and it may be. there was
some truth in the charge of the. Maso party, that - the
election was fixed; beforehand and that for 'them to
take part in it would be a farce.
DISPATCHES from Cuba announce that in al
most, all election districts the Maso party
fulfilled their threat of ignoring. the election
and withdrew their candidates for Presidential and
Senatorial electors. The result .was a light vote arid
the overwhelming success of the party of Palma.
While, therefore, the formal election of the President
does not take place until next month, Cuba has vir
tually chosen her first chief magistrate and is about
to enter upon the experiment of self-government.
CUBA'S FIRST PRESIDENT.
A Philadelphia millionaire helped out the Christ
mas merriment in his set by giving a "Klondike
dinner." Everybody had to eat from tin plates and
drink from the bottle. It was, in fact, just -such a
dipner as none but a millionaire could afford to give,
and shows what a nice and economical thing it is to
be rich. .
Southern California has made effective use of "the
excellence r of her winter climate as a means of at
tracting settlers. San Francisco and the district
round the bay should follow the example. We have
never advertised as we should the advantages of the
winter climate of this \ section of the ' State, and as a
consequence we. do not receive anything like the win
ter travel: from the East [. that T : .we tshiould.M^The^
frolicsome ' spirit; of gayety that ; manifests, itself '- in'
this city every New Year's eve suggests -the possibil
ity of ; a carnival at this season which would rival "if
not eclipse the -Mardi . , Gras ifestiyities of New Or
leans; and it would doubtless- repay us well to unde>- Â¦
take such- a celebration; Certainly .our â¢ midwinter;
climate deserves more attention -than we give = it. ,
It is to note the success of festivals like
this. They serve. the double purpose" of providing a
joyous holiday for all who are in reach of them and
at the same time they demonstrate and advertise the
delights of our midwinter climate.; With ' each suc
cessive fete of the kind a new proof is given that it is
in California that nature -has provided the happiest
habitation for man. Here it is that < the soil yields
most 'to his labor and the climate is most propitious
to his enjoyment. . .
.'Â¦ The descriptions of the day and of the sports will
seerii to readers in the frozen East like a fairy tale of
tropic lands. The sky was clear, the air was warm,'
and while, there was nonsuch profusion of flowers .as
in former years, there were' still blossoms enough -of
all kinds to make the festival glov; with beauty sur
passing anything, the East sees even in the glory of

nia during^the winter,; and has furnished the

Having formerly resided near Norwich I can
answer the query as to the steamer asked
about by C. F. F. The name of the ill-fated'
steamer was the Atlantic, commanded by Cap
tain Hllliard. She left Allyn's Point three

train from Boston, via. Worcester, with pas
sengers for New York.~ The steamer left the
wharf at ; 10 o'clock at night, a very strong
gale from .the northwest .blowing. at the time.
Daniel Webster was one of the passengers on
j the i train from Boston, but . decided to remain
over until the next day. fearing that some dis
aster might occur. When the Atlantic Â¦was
about five miles off New London light thÂ«
steam Â¦_ chest burst and she became- helpless;
both" anchors were let j go, but the steamer

heavy sea that was raging, and at' 9 o'clock
November" 25. .1846 (Thanksgiving day),' she
went upon the rocks at the. west end of Fish
er's; Island. Â¦ But few of the, eighty: or more
passengers were saved. Captain Hilliard went
down with his vessel. .

A strong guild of classicists yet holds its ground in
our universities, but the children of the people are
turning to the studies which increase the power of
man over nature, and not his knowledge of a dead
language and the blind staggering of the races that
thought in it and were without knowledge of the
physical universe of which they were a part
The useful man of these days does not patter Latin.
He is at work upon the dynamic problems which af
fect the welfare of the race. He has applied power
and has derived it from sources unknown in that age
when learning consisted in reading Virgil, Caesar and
the literature of the Augustan age in the original: .
Learning now has its best application in the study
of man and his needs. Compare our scientific agri
culture, with its analysis of soils, its breeding of
plants, its vegetable physiology and pathology, its
hybridizing, its adaptation of food to desired results,
with the bucolics, and the dependence of man upon
the practical is revealed.
Since" then practically the whole of physics has
been born, and its application to materialities is that
great body of applied science which distinguishes the
modern* world. . ' ' -V
Latin has been made a fetich because in the
middle ages, and long subsequently, to know it , was
accepted as evidence of great learning and accom
plishment . But when it was advanced to the head
ship of studies physics was practically unknown. At
that time the world had alchemy instead of- chemistry
and astrology instead of astronomy^ and the nobler
uses of mathematics were unknown.
t-x RESIDENT JORDAN, of Stanford has ex-
I â ' pressed the thoughts of many educators in de
Â«Â¦â¢ moting Latin from the primacy it has too long
enjoyed in the curriculum of the schools and univer- j
sities. A knowledge of its grammatical structure
may be acquired, to serve as information upon its
derivatives incorporated into the English tongue, but
to make it the leading part of a scholastic course is '
to divert attention and take time from learning that
is far more important to the modern man or woman.
Dr. Jordan is himself in the front rank of American
scholars, but how small a part of his eminence is due
to his Latin! * '. Â¦ . ' y * 'i:',

There are other seals of various shapes and sizes belong
ing to other imperial palaces, courts or pavilions, but the
above brief description, .suffices .to show the uses to which
they are put by the Empress Dowager. In fact, the Interest
in these seals does not He in the material of which they are
made (green-colored jadestone), but because they were the
personal property of the now famous Empress Dowager, Tze-
Hsi Tuan-Yu, etc., the present real ruler of China.
writing or painting a scroll she used thesÂ© seals on It to show
where it was done. There Is another pair of similarly shaped
seals .belonging to the Lichinghslen Court, one of which has
dragons and the other phoenixes carved on the borders. As a
matter of fact, the phoenix is the special mark or crest of aa
Empress or Empress Dowager of the Chinese empire, while
the dragon is the mark of the Emperor. In the present case,
however, the Empress Dowager, having usurped, the reins of
government, arrogated to herself the prerogatives of the Em
peror, and â¢ therefore she had dragons carved on the borders
of one of these seals of the Ldchinghsien Court. This makes
the pair of seals belonging to that court the most Interesting
of the whole lot of the Empress Dowager's seals, and will bo
a token -to succeeding generations of her usurpation and tha
reality of her power in the empire.
Of these jadestone seals the most beautifully carved are a
pair of rectangular-shaped ones" with phoenixes carved on the
borders, while the characters in the middle denote that they
belonged to the Yik'un Palaceâ that is. to say, when the Em
press Dowager happened to be in the Yik'un Palace while

. The seals in question are of different sizes, some square
and some rectangular, and the characters (ancient Chinese
seal characters) on them denote to what throne hall, pavilion
or kiosk In the palaces each belongs. 'It is a well known fact
that the Empress Dowager was very fond, of painting In water
colors and writing large characters, : some as much as a N foot
and a. half square in size, which she liked, to hang up in the
different palaces and imperial apartments as a proof of her
ability In this line, and which she Intended to be transmitted
to posterity. 'On each of these scrolls of paintings or writ
ten characters her Majesty pressed one of her own jadestone
seals to' show to the world that these scrolls were by her own
imperial hand. . '-^T.'*. :

brilliant^ capital on .a o'uli salary list. Congress, there
fore might as well put an end to the agony and pass
the salary bills. They will help to, reduce the % surplus
and to keep it from occurring again.
While . the names of the officials quoted are not
given it .is probable the stories are true. Washing
ton has ceased to be a simple country town where a
man with $8000 a year and perquisites could afford
to entertain his friends. It has become one of the
most extravagant and expensive capitals in the
world: No: matter how simple may be the ideas and
how frugal the habits of the man who goes to Wash
ington to :serve the Government, he soon gets I high
ideas. The s first time he meets* an Embassador he
feels that he .'must raise" his style of living to the Em
bassadoriai: level. The Cabinet officers are not the
only ones ; affected. ---The desire for; higher pay and
a larger life. runs all along, the line; The Senators^
for example, are; not asking for more pay directly^
but they are discussing a .scheme to get the Govern
ment to furnish each of them with. two committee
rooms. By and;by they will demand an official Sen
atorial residence with a salary to fit it."

OFFICIAL SALARIES. 7
Charles C. Dawes, former 1 Comptroller' of the Cur
rency, says that ithe -Â¦'next Presidential campaign will
bejfought on the .issue of "asset currency," so the
spell binders' might as well get^ in and begin to. study
ther subject so as to-be -able to illustrate ' it on the
stump with entertaining, anecdotes.
The United States does not own Cuba, but it does
own the Philippines and will maintain a tariff on
Philippine sugar. A policy thatjshuts out Philippine
sugar by a tariff and lets Cuban sugar, in free would
be a sight for gods and men, and would be matter
for hooting in a campaign.
Applying its misrepresentation of Mr. . Oxnard's
statement of conditions under the free sugar of the
McKinley bill, the junta says: "Cuba knocks at our
door asking help.- Why should it be denied? Have
we not assumed a protectorate over the island? Do
we not practically direct its government? Cuban in
terests are, ours, and w"e should deal with Cuba on a
basis of broad liberality commensurate with the
power and; generosity of this great republic. It is
for the United States to make or unmake Cuba. Per
petuation of the present raw sugar tariff on imports
from that island means its ruination. Should we ac
cept such responsibility? It is on record and beyond
j question that the domestic beet sugar industry, is a
' self-supporting and profitable enterprise without the
aid of Government protection." The "record" re
ferred to is what" Mr. Oxnard said about it when it
received a bounty of 2 cents a "pound;- the 'fact being
that beet sugar has never been. a single hour without
protection., either by tariff or bounty, so that there
is not one hour's experience to prove theassertion
of the junta that it is profitable without protection.
but lying is a vice.
While assailing Mr. Oxnard's consistency the junta
should tell the truth. Inconsistency may be 'a folly,
All of this is put out to allay the fears of American
sugar producers and in the hope that they will forget
that Mr. Oxnard's statement wasjaased on the fact
that while the McKinley bill admitted raw slgar free
it" also paid a bounty of 2 cents a pound on domestic
sugar, beet, cane and maple.
Some of these have decided to make a personal is
sue of Mr. Henry Oxnard, who has been and isj a
leading promoter of American beet sugar. It is not
necessary to defend the consistency of Mr. Oxnard's
statistics as a promoter and investor with his argu
ments against free trade in sugar. Nor is it necessary
to vindicate him as the beginning or the end^ot wis
dom on the subject. The farmers, who have felt the
relief of a new use. for their land, and the benefit of
a new crop more profitable than any of their former'
staples, have ideas on the subject which do not at all
depend on anything Mr. Oxnard has done or omitted.
Yet the junta seems to wish to go to trial on, the."
consistency of Mr. Oxnard. To this, end it is ex
pensively circulating a statement by him that, under
the McKinley bill, which admitted raw sugar free, the
lowest average price for sugar was 4 cents a pound,
being a reduction of 2.17 cents a pound from the ave
rage of the year before that law took effect. The
junta says: "But tint did not dismay Promoter Ox
nard a little bit. Indeed he went on to show how,
even at the free trade price, the beet men could pros
per amazingly. His experience. showed that^ conser
vatively estimated," beets would .yield 250 j pounds
granulated sugar per ton. This at 4 cents a pound
meant $10 a ton for. every ton of beets marketed. The
cost of the beets was $4 per ton, the cost of working â¢
them into sugar was $3 per ton. This would leave
a net profit of $3 per ton."
Seeing this, the Post enlists under the banner of
the junta and fights! he' battle of free trade from prin
ciple. Knowing its motive one . can easily respect its
tactical acuteness. But we may not respect, for that
or any other reason, other organs and agencies of the
junta. * . . ,
THE New York Evening Post, free trade, nat
urally supports the Cuban junta in its proposed
removal of protection from the^ American sugar
and tobacco growers. It would with 'equal energy
support free tin, plate glass, silk and the numerous
other articles which have -been domesticated in this
country by protection. . The free traders are wise in
their day and generation: . They .know' that the
strength of protection lies in- its support by the farm
ers of this country. Withdraw that and the -system
will" fall. : r They know, too, that the best way to turn
the farmers against protection is to deprive them of
it. When they are no longer in a system which can
justify itself only in', its equal application to agricul
ture and manufactures they will resent its use as a
means of extending. Government partiality and favor
to one class of industries at the expense of -another
class. â¢ ... " . . , , â¢Â¦â¢â¢Â¦Â¦Â¦',

MOBE WRECKAGE REPORTED.
While there the Indiana and Pennsyl
vania have been, remodeled. Nearly all
of the cabin accommodations have been
removed and new decks put in. The boil
ers have been renewed and the refriger
ating plants put in the very best of order.
During the war each vessel carried more
than 1500 soldiers in an emergency, be
sides 200 cabin passengers. From now on
they will only have room for forty cabin
passengers each and no soldiers, all the
room being set aside for freight. During
the remodeling of the Indiana and Penn
sylvania the Ohio has been used as a
boarding-house. The machinists and car
penters and boiler-makers from the works
have lived aboard her and at the same
time given her an overhauling. The Ohio
has not been altered as far as cabin ac
commodations are concerned, and she
will be the passenger vessel of the fleet.
The work of getting the Albion ready
for the interisland trade Is now being
rushed, and the chances are that as soon
as she is ready for her run to Manila the
Ohio, Indiana and Pennsylvania will be
ready to begin their' trips. All three ves
sels are of the same dimensions, but vary
somewhat in tonnage^wing to the differ
ence in their fittings. They are 343 feet
long, 43 feet beam and 24 feet 9 inches
deep. - Â¦
The Ohio. Indiana and Pennsylvania
are well known in transport service.
They came here atahe beginning of the
war to enter the Klondike trade, but the
United States was then securing every
available vessel to carry soldiers to the
Philippines, so the steamships were at
once chartered. Being very handy craft,
they were kept in the interisland service,
carrying soldiers and supplies all through
the war. About six months ago they
came back to San Francisco and have
since been laid up at the Union Iron
Works.
The line will be established, however,
and by the Empire Steamship Company.
That concern is acting In conjunction with
the persons who purchased the Albion a
few days ago. It will put on the run be
tween San Francisco and Manila the
steamsfilps Ohio, Indiana and Pennsylva
nia, while the Albion will run in the in
terlsland trade.
FOR months past there have been
rumors of a merchant line of
steamships from this port to Ma
nila- Some people said the Pacific
Mall Steamship Company would
put on a fleet and others asserted that a
syndicate would purchase the Govern
ment transports and run them as an in
dependent line. The Pacific Mall is making
no move in the direction of a Philippine
service and the transports - are here to
stay as long as Uncle Sam wants them
to carry his soldiers and their belongings
to and from the seat of trouble in his
latest possession.

NEWS OF THE OCEAN".
On petition of interested property own
ers the Board of Works was requested to
notify Cushing, Wetmore : & Co. to dis
charge\blasts of less size on Telegraph
Hill, so that adjacent property will not
be damaged. -. :
Joseph Rothschild opposed the improve
ment, contending that it will "villagize"
the city to plant trees > in the center of
streets. , Robert Ewing. Charles Acker
man and W. L. Bradbury addressed the
committee in favor of the project. Acker
man , stated that when Euclid avenue, in
Cleveland, was improved in a similar
manner property values increased from
$30 to $2000 per front foot. -
James Cavanagh and William. Redpath
objected, to granting permission to the
Chutes Company to erect a bridge across
Tenth avenue, and the matter went over
to allow the committee to visit the prem
ises in company with petitioners and pro
testants..' Charles Ackerman explained
that the bridge was a necessity as an
additional means of emergency exit from
the theater and a safeguard to public life.
. The Supervisors' Street Committee yes
terday reported In favor of the passage
of a bill repealing the franchise granted
the Bay and Coast Railway Company to
enter the city at Twenty-fourth and Illi
nois streets. Action was taken on the
report of the Board of Public Works that
the company has failed ' to comply with
the conditions imposed in its charter. The
company was required to expend $25,000
in construction work within a period of
two years, bu^ has not done so.
The bill providing for the adornment of
Van Ness avenue, between Bush and
Pine streets, was amended to provide for
the construction of granite or cement
curbs on the sidewalks, as the Board - of
Works may determine, and also to state
that the project is intended as an experi
ment and that in case of a general ex
pression of Â¦ disapproval from Van Ness
avenue property owners the board will
restore the roadway to its original condi
tion. â¢ ; ; â¢â¢
This Is the first case of the sort pros
ecuted by the electric, company. Others
suspected of tapping the wires are under
surveillance. â¢
Beck brought some witnesses, among
them Policeman Thomson and a special
officer, - to testify that his incandescent
lights were put out each night about " 9
o'clock and the two arc lights about 10:;w
o'clock. Beck testified , that he had got
an electrician named Peacock .to put
wires in his store for the sake of econ
omy, but denied tapping the wires him
self or knowing that they were tapped.
He admitted ln cross-examination that he
burned thirty-seven incandescent llghu
and two arc lights, and when shown that
his bills -never exceeded .$11 per month
and were as low as $6 be could not give
a ' satisfactory explanation of his plea of
economy. .... Â¦"
Beck was convicted by the jury, which
was out but a short time.
. The principal witness for the prosecu
tion was Seth Cohen, inspector of meters
for the, company. Last November Cohen j
examined Beck'3 premises and after a I
careful search he discovered that , the
company's wires had been cut and tapped
by two wires that fed thirty-eight in
candescent lights which were not regis
tered at the meter. The connections were
made ln what was supposed to be a store
room. Beck's contract witn the company
called for live incandescent and two arc
lights. Cohen was corroborated by G. W.
Carroll, another employe of tho company.
William O. PennycooK, inspector in tne
city department ot electricity, testined to
having seen on July 'a last about forty
Incandescent lignta burning in Beck's
store. He asked Butler, the clerk, wno
had done the job, and Butler replied "tha
boss." Butler was instructed to tell
Beck to call at- the office of the depart
ment, but he,,flid not do so. W. R. Hew
itt, chief electrician in the department ot
electricity, also testified that Beck told
him he had done the work himself. Other
witnesses examined were Frank Bride
man . of the same department, Jesse
Mayer and Ward A. 'I>wight Jr. of -the
Independent Company, and George C.
Butler, Beck's clerk.

Captain Christian Y. Fosen is not going
out on the Grace Dollar this trip and
Captain Johnson takes his place. Instead
Captain Fosen will go out as chief^ officer
of the new steamer Melville Dollar under
Captain Gow. In spite of the apparent
"Irish "raise," Captain Fosen was the
proudest man on the front. HeJs wear
ing the handsomest gold watch and chain
that money could buy and it came from
his employers. He is the commodore of
the Dollar fleet and Captain Gow is new
to the coast. When Mr. Dollar asked him
if he would give the new master all the
information possible Fosen at once con
sented. Turning to Captain Gow he ask
ed, "Have you a full crew?" and the an
swer was, "No, I lack a first mate." "All
right," said Fosen, "I'll ship as first mate
and make the run with you." This spirit
cf willingness so pleased Mr. Dollar that
the watch and chain presentation fol
lowed.
Captain Charles Minor Goodall goes
East to-day to attend the stockholders'
meeting of the Pacific Coast Company
end the Pacific Coast Steamship Com
pany. He will be gone several weeks.

DOMESTIC PORTS.
The Police Commission sent a communi
cation yesterday to the Board of Super
visors, stating that in : its opinion the
operation of the so-called nickel-in-the-.
slot machines, which .ordinance No. 78 as
sumes to -license, is unlawful, as being
prohibited by section 330 of the Penal
Code, and that the ordinance is void as
being ln conflict with the State law.

COMPANY LOSES
ITS FRANCHISE
Four Italians who own a small rajich
at Rio Vista, on the Sacramento, had a
falling out on the water front yesterday.
They came to the city to sell their es
tate and quarreled over the distribution
of the proceeds. Antonio Baroni. think
ing he was getting the worst of the deal
at the hands of Fairo Bandoni, drew a
pistol and began shooting. - Alphonso Pe
tretti rushed In to save Bandoni and was
shot in the shoulder. He was taken to
the Harbor Hospital, where his wound
was dressed, while Baroni was taken to
the Harbor police station and charged
with assault to commit murder.

To Leave for Philippines.
The Oregon Railway and. Navigation
Company's Columbia, which was ashore
In the Columbia last Tuesday, arrived in
port yesterday. The passengers never left
the vessel and Captain Doran says she
was never in any danger. With the as
sistance of the river steamer Harvest
Queen, she got off after discharging SCO
tons of cargo. The run down the coast
was uneventful. Captain Doran said:
"We went ashore twenty miles south
of Portland and escaped without injury.
We ran on to a sandbar and after lighter
ing, some of the cargo we got off. There
was no excitement, as there was no dan
ger. The ship is all rjght." l

Shipping men and the underwriters fear
that this wreckage is from the British
ship Hyderabad, now out 69 days from
Newcastle, Australia, for San Francisco.
She is a very fast vessel and on her last
trip here made the run in 57 days. A
boat of hers was picked up by a tug some
weeks ago and it Is now thought the ves
sel has come to grief in the recent gales
that have prevailed on the Pacific,
Off the Columbia River the Aorangl
sighted a- schooner bottom up. The
schooner Reliance is now out 42 days from
San Francisco for Coquille River, and
many people think she is the unfortunate
craft. Still the Reliance may have been
driven off shore, and her,owners have not
given up hope.

While in Milan Manager Leahy - will
study the architecture and seating plans
of its grand opera-houses with a view to
ward incorporating the ideas thus gained
in the plans for the new Tivoli which is
to be built in this city ; next' year. He
will also get some ' ideas about a Verdi
statue, for the erection of which money
is now being raised in this city. Alto
gether "Doc", has a big contract on ins
hands and it is likely that he will crowd
much activity Â¦ into his life during the
next two months.
In Milan I will see Riccordi, thr great pub
lisher of operas, and- arrange with him for
productions of such works as "La Tosca," "Fe
dora," "Andre Chenier" and others of that
class. ; When I have Â¦ completed . my work ln
Italy I will go to Paris and London. . In the
English capital I will see George Edwards re
garding numerous light operas which have been
produced on the Continent, but never seen here.
When I return I expect to be in possession of
a musical treasure trove which will delight our
patrons for months to come. I expect to return
home about March 1.
I shall leave San Francisco ln time. to catch
the Italian steamer Liguria, which leaves New
York for Naples on the 16th inst. I shall then
make trips to Rome, Milan, Florence and other
cities - and attend the great theaters of those
cities in which the presentation of grand opera
is exclusive. My purpose is to hear, the best
vocalists and witness the : presentation of the
best grand operas.. In this way I shall be en
abled to secure just such artists and operas
aa will be suited to the lovers of the best music
in Â§an Francisco. I will sign no less than eight
singers of both sexes and they shall be the best
that money can procure. â¢ .
. It is the; custom of the Tivoli manage
ment to hold a grand opera season each
fall at which the latest operas are
presented by the great European ' artists.
â¢It is, true such stars as Calve, Eames and
Sembrich have not yet been seen at the
Tivoli, . but that is ? not the fault of , the
enterprising management. Previous en
gagements alone rendered it impossible
for , them , to close contracts .with "Doc,"
but in their "stead such vocalists as Col
lamarini, â¢ Salassa and others Â¦ have been
secured to the delight of the Tivoli pat
rons, "Whose musical education is not a
whit inferior to that of the average per
son whom a Grau season at $7 a seat
serves to throw into spasms of artistic
fervor. '.< It is this disposition of the Tivoli
management to give the best for the least
money that has ; contributed so largely to
the success and. fame of- that house and
has served to keep the popular taste for
the best works of world-famed composers
whetted for more. :
Manager Leahy's purpose- in making the
trip to Italy is to secure ' several vocal
stars and the best and latest : composi
tions of renowned" composers. In explain
ing the objects of his journey to the Old
World Manager Leahy said: .
Genial William H. Leahy, business man
ager of ; the Tivoli, will leave for Italy
next week, on a â¢mission': of-. Bupreme im
portance to the Â¦" patrons : of that' popular
house. It lsbecause of his contemplated
journey that "Doc" has been studying in
secret one of those works the perusal . of
which will enable one, to master at a sin
gle breath the intricacies of a foreign
tongue, .but .which ; in, his case has re
sulted in mystifying: him more and more
as he advanced, until now he 7 sadly con
fesses Jthat; his Italian vocabulary is lim
ited to . Vslgnore" and ', "signora," with -a
"si".- thrown in for company's sake. Â¦

Dried Fruit in New York.
Mail advices from New York say; "The
market is very quiet at all points at the mo
ment and trade from now until after January
1 is likely to be trifling. The general tone is
strong, however, and with epot stocks moder
ate an active and healthy condition is looked
for during the early month of 1902. Prunes
are firmer on the coast, new Santa Claras be
ing more generally held at a 3%c basis. Some
eizes are still available %c less. Some business
was reported in email Elzes at a 3%c basis,
t. o. b. coast. Visalias were quoted from the
coast yesterday at a 3c basis, but some va
rieties other than Santa Claras, it is under
stood, still offer at %c less. Oregons are most
ly held at a 2%c basis, this being an advance
of lc over the price which prevailed not many
â¢weeks back. Raisins continue withdrawn by
nearly all packers in California, but some still
quote loose Muscatels on the basis of the last
advance. New prices will be made by the
seeding combine, it is expected, after the first
of the year. Currants are firm at 5%c for
Amalias on the spot, sale of 100 barrels being
made at that figure. Goods to arrive per Mlr
amlr, now due, are mostly held at 5%c.
Peaches and apricots are Bteady but very dull.
Evaporated apples are quiet.' raspberries easier
and blackberries ecarce and wanted."

New York Stock Market:
NEW YORK, Jan.>2.â The first day of trad
ing of the new year on the Stock Exchange was
signalized by a Jump in the volume of trad-
Ing to upward of a million shares. The coal
pti. and the Pacifies were easily the leaders
in the market, but the dealings were broadly
distributed and the Â¦ strength was fairly dis
seminated through ' the general list. The In
dustrial stocks were inconspicuous except for
a few of the most important issues, euch aa
United States Steel. Sugar and 'Amalgamated
Copper and People's Gas. Amalgamated Cop
per had a period of weakness during the early
part of the day and Sugar showed occasional
relapses. There was very heavy realizing in
the course of the day, but the offerings were
well taken and at a cost of on.ly fractional
losses in the prices. The way in which one
stock or group of stocks and then another was
brought forward into the advance was char
acteristic of the methods of professional oper
ators of large caliber and as each new point of
strength developed the preceding stocks were
sold to realize. In this way the equipoise
was maintained and speculative holdings were
transferred from one quarter to another, per
meating the whole market. The demand was
apparently . based on general considerations of
a good prospect of continued prosperity of the
country for the new year and the hope of an
early relaxation in the money market Call
loar; rates were not so Etringent as on Monday
and Tuesday, but the money market continued
stiff and the bulk of operations was appar
ently by those not dependent for resources on
the call loan market. The Inspired intima
tions of the completion of the retirement of
Northern Pacific preferred and of the rapid
progress of exchange Into the Northern Securi
ties Company's stock had a notable effect in
relieving the depression " which . has been
caused by the fears that that project would
be obstructed before its successful consum
mation. The payment ; for redemption of
Northern Pacific preferred was believed to be
a factor in relieving the money market. The
prospect of a further effect from the large
January disbursements was an additional mo
tive in the speculation. With the Northern
Pacific project out of the way, it is believed
that other projects for combinations . will be
pushed forward.' It is confidently believed
that the anthracite coalers are destined for
euch a combination. The speculation in this
group and the Pacifies to-day may be at
tributed to thÂ«>se causes. The buying of Balti
more & Ohio was by the same brokers con
spicuous in the operations In the anthracite
group.
Amalgamated Copper was helped by the firm
ness of the raw copper market in London and
by rumors that a powerful syndicate had been
formed to hold the company's accumulated
stocks of copper. The closing was active and
easy on realizations.
The railway bond market was active and
broad and there was a good absorption of high
grade bonds. Total sales, $4,640,000.
United States refunding 2s and the old 4s
declined % and the 5s advanced % per cent
on the last call. ' .

FORECAST.
WEATHER CONDITIONS . AND GENERAL
The pressure has risen steadily over the
entire country west of the Rocky Mountains
and has fallen slowly through the Rocky
Mountain region. The storm on the Oregon
coast has moved northward, but may recurve.
Rain has fallen in moderate quantities from
Cape Mendocino northward.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, January 3; 1902:
San Francisco and vicinity â Cloudy Friday,
light northerly, changing to fresh southerly,
winds. ALEXANDER G. McADIE.
Forecast Official.
Northern California â Cloudy and unsettled
weather Friday, probably rain in northern por
tion; fresh southerly wind.
Southern California â Cloudy Friday; light
westerly winds.
Nevada â Cloudy Friday: light southerly
winds.

During the Civil "War O'Connor fought
with the Sixty-ninth New York Regi
ment.
OAKLAND, Jan. 2.â E. D. O'Connor, a
veteran of the Civil War, fell ten feet to
the bottom of Strawberry Creek that runs
through the university grounds at Berke
ley. The old man, who is partially, par
alyzed, ! lost his way in the darkness and
walked oft a bluff into the creek. He
lay there until found at daylight and wa3
in a weakened condition as a result of
the exposure. Ho was removed to the
greenhouse of the university, where he
remained until Town Marshal Kerns made
arrangements to have him sent to the
Receiving Hospital in Oakland, j Dr. Wil
liams, who attended -him, found that he
had sustained an abrasion of the scalp,
but that there were no broken bones.

DETROIT, Mich., Jan. 2.â Miss Beulah
Wheeler, a beautiful young society
woman, died early to-day at her home on
Second avenue from arsenic poisoning.
Last evening she retired to her room to
rest before starting for the theater. Soon
afterward she called her mother for aid
and said she had taken the wrong medi
cine. What the young woman had taken
for antlpyrine proved to be arsenic. Miss
Wheeler, who was 22 years of age, was
born in Rome, N. Y.

Thomas Estrada Palma. the Nationalist
candidate for the Presidency of Cuba, has
nfty-five electors, while General Baito
lome Maso, the Democratic candidate,
who withdrew from the compaign kas
eight electors. Senor Palma secured the
unanimous electoral delegations of the
provinces of Pinar del Rio, Havana,
Matanzas and Santa Clara, and one elec
tor from Puerte Principe and five electors
from Santiago. General Maso secured
three electors from Puerto Principe and
live from Santiago.
The following civil Governors were
elected: Perez in Pinar del Rio; Nunez
in Havana; Lecuona in Matanzas; Gomez
in Santa Clara; Recio in Puerto Prin
cipe, and Echevarria in Santiago
province.

The Grocers' Fruit Growing Company is
suing the Kern County Land Company to
recover a valuable tract of land in Kern
County. The plaintiffs claim that they
acquired the 500 acres in dispute from
the land company in March, 1896, for $35,
000, the sum to be paid in installments.
They made a number of improvements "on
the tract. Because the plaintiffs could
not pay the installments they gave the
property back to the defendants, they say,
with the agreement that all the profits de
rived from the ranch should be credited
to them so that they could regain posses
sion of the land. It is now alleged that
because of the increase in value of the
property the defendant company displays
an unwillingness to carry out the agree
ment. ' â¢â¢ â¢ .
Two Companies Claim , Ranch.

evade the law.

Mrs. Lyndall was picked up by a stran
ger. She told him her name, but declared
that she lived in Santa Clara. He aroused
Mrs. Schneider, who had difficulty in be
lieving that the sorely crippled form was
that of her mother' she had tucked in
bed a few hours previously.
Dr. Edward R. Sill was called in and
everything possible done for the injured
woman. Her right hip was fractured
and serious internal injury sustained. 1
Sho retained consciousness, however, un
til this morning, when she died.
Mrs. Lyndall has. not resided in Santa
Clara for more than thirty years. The
shock of the fatal fair rendered blank
the interval since leaving there, and she
died under the impression that she was
still in Santa Clara among the friends of
thirty years ago. .
She was a native of New York. She
leaves a daughter, with whom she lived,
and a son, C. P. Lyndall, who is in the
insurance business in Los Angeles. Her
husband was killed in an accident at
Union City some twenty years ago.
When picked up, probably about two
hours after falling, she had clutched in
her hand one of the pillows from her bed
and was feeling about for the covers and
complaining of the cold. i Â¦
Deceased was a widow and lived , with
her daughter, Mrs. J. H. Schneider, at
611 East Fifteenth street. The old lady
i:as been ailing for some time and her
mental faculties were considerably im
paired. Â¦; She was - extremely feeble j and,
required -constant watching.'
She was seen safely to bed on Monday
evening, but evidently arose during the
night and. lost her bearings in the darjj
ness." The < window through which she
fell was open only about eighteen inches.
' Under the impression that she was
climbing into bed' Mrs. Elizabeth Lyn
dall, 77 years of age, last Monday night
crawled through a second-story window,
fell to the ground and received injuries
from which' she died this morning.
